Thy love hath passed away from me,

Left desolate, forlorn,

In winter-rains how wearily,

Thy summer past I mourn!

Your love has passed away from me and left me desolate.  I mourn how wearily the summer passed away in winter rains.

My humble hut is thatched with straw from field where rice-sheaves scattered. Now autumn`s harvest was over, gathered by my hand. The sky is seen through thatched roof and my clothes are wet with dew.

 

ç§ã®ç²—æœ«ãªã‚ãšã¾ã‚„ã¯ã€ç¨²æŸãŒæ•£ã‚‰ã°ã£ã¦ã„ã‚‹ç”°ã‚“ã¼ã‹ã‚‰ã¨ã£ãŸè—ã§è‘ºã‹ã‚Œã¦ã„ã‚‹ã€‚ä»Šã€ç§‹ã®åç©«ãŒçµ‚ã‚ã£ãŸãŒã€ãã‚Œã¯ç§ã®æ‰‹ã§é›†ã‚ãŸã‚‚ã®ã ã€‚ã‚ã‚‰ã¶ãå±‹æ ¹ã®ã™ãé–“ã‹ã‚‰ç©ºãŒè¦‹ãˆã¦ã€ç§ã®ç€ç‰©ã¯éœ²ã«æ¿¡ã‚Œã¦ã„ã‚‹ã€‚

 

humb e ç²—æœ«ãª ã€€ hu t æ±å±‹ ã€€ be thatched with ... ãƒ»ãƒ»ãƒ»ã§è‘ºã‹ã‚Œã¦ã„ã‚‹ ã€€ stra w ã‚ã‚‰ ã€€ rice-sheave s ç¨²ã®æŸ ã€€ scatte r æ’’ãæ•£ã‚‰ã™ ã€€ de w ã€€éœ²

 


My lowly hut is thatched with straw
From fields where rice-sheaves frequent stand, 
Now autumn`s harvest well-night o`er, 
Collected by my toiling hand: 
Tatter`d roof the sky I view, 
My clothes are wet with falling dew.

(Translated by Dickins)


Coarse the rush-mat roof 
Sheltering the harvest-hut 
Of the autumn rice-field;-- 
And my sleeves are growing wet 
With the moisture dripping through.

(Translated by MacCauley)

The pleasant spring has passed away and the heavenly summit of Mont. Kaku may be seen in all its grandeur.  

In long time past the drying ground was whitened with robes, spread around.

The pleasant spring hath passed away, 
Now summer follows close, I ween, 
And Ama`s secret summit may 
In all its grandeur now be seen; 
Of yore the drying ground, 
Whitened with angels` robes, spread far around.

(Translated by Dickins)


Spring, it seems, has passed, 
And the summer come again; 
For the silk-white robes, 
So `tis said, are spread to dry 
On the "Mount of Heaven`s Perfume."

(Translated by MacCauley)

The hillside fowl with its long tail sweeps over the ground.  I mourn my loneliness through so long night and I bewail my sleepless wretchedness.

 

é•·ã„å°¾ã®ã‚ã‚‹å±±é³¥ãŒåœ°é¢ã‚’æƒãã‚ˆã†ã«æ­©ãã€‚ç§ã¯é•·ã„å¤œã®é–“ã˜ã‚…ã†å­¤ç‹¬ã‚’å˜†ãæ‚²ã—ã¿ã€çœ ã‚Œãªã„å¤œã®æ‚©ã¿ã‚’ã‹ã“ã£ã¦ã„ã‚‹ã€‚

 

èªå¥ ã€€ hillsid e å±±è…¹ ã€€ fow l å¤§å‹ã®é³¥ ã€€ swee p æƒãã€æƒãã‚ˆã†ã«å¼•ããšã‚‹ ã€€ mour n å˜†ãæ‚²ã—ã‚€ ã€€ lonelines s å¯‚ã—ã•ã€å­¤ç‹¬ ã€€ bewai l å˜†ã ã€€ sleeples s çœ ã‚Šã®ãªã„ã€çœ ã‚Œãªã„ ã€€ wretchednes s ã€€ã¿ã˜ã‚ã•ã€è‹¦æ‚©

 


The hill-side fowl his long dropped tail,

Sweeps o`er the ground -- so drags the night.

My lonely plight

I mourn -- my sleepless wretchedness bewail.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Ah! the foot-drawn trail

Of the mountain-pheasant`s tail

Drooped like down-curved branch!--

Through this long, long-dragging night

Must I keep my couch alone?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

On going out to the Tago`s shore, the wandering eye may roam over Fuji Mountain whose lofty summit is whitened with the newly fallen snow.

 

ç”°å­ã®æµ¦ã®å²¸ã¹ã«å‡ºãŸç¬é–“ã€ã•ã¾ã‚ˆãˆã‚‹è¦–ç·šã‚‚ã€ã¯ã‚‹ã‹é«˜ã„é ‚ä¸ŠãŒæ–°ã—ãé™ã£ãŸé›ªã§ç™½ããªã£ã¦ã„ã‚‹å¯Œå£«å±±ã®ä¸Šã«æ³¨ãŒã‚Œã‚‹ã ã‚ã†ã€‚

 

èªå¥ ã€€ shor e å²¸è¾º ã€€ wande r ã•ã¾ã‚ˆã†ã€ãã‚‡ã‚ãã‚‡ã‚ã™ã‚‹ ã€€ roa m ã¶ã‚‰ã¤ãã€æ­©ãå›ã‚‹ ã€€ loft y éå¸¸ã«é«˜ã„ã€ãã³ãˆã¦ã„ã‚‹


From where my home, -

My lonely home, - on Tago`s shore

Doth stand, the wandering eye may roam

O`er Fusiyama`s summit hoar,

Whose lofty brow

Is whitened by th` new-fallen snow.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


When to Tago`s coast

I the way have gone, and see

Perfect whiteness laid

On Mount Fuji`s lofty peak

By the drift of falling snow.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Now amid the hills fallen maple leaves are trampled down beneath deer`s hoofs whose cries are heard far and near.  My shivering heart chills with autumn`s affection of flame. 

 

ä»Šã€å±±ã®ä¸­ã§ã€ã‚«ã‚¨ãƒ‡ã®è½ã¡è‘‰ãŒé¹¿ã®ã²ã¥ã‚ã«è¸ã¿ã—ã‚ã‚‰ã‚Œã¦ã„ã‚‹ãŒã€ãã®é¹¿ã®é³´ãå£°ãŒé ãã«ã‚‚è¿‘ãã«ã‚‚èã“ãˆã‚‹ã€‚ç‚ã®ã‚ˆã†ãªç§‹ã®æ„›ã«éœ‡ãˆã‚‹ç§ã®å¿ƒã‚‚å¯’ã€…ã¨ãªã‚‹ã€‚

 

èªå¥ ã€€ ami d çœŸã£åªä¸­ã§ã€çœŸã‚“ä¸­ã§ ã€€ mapl e ã‹ãˆã§ ã€€ trampl e è¸ã¿ã¤ã‘ã‚‹ ã€€ beneat h ã®ä¸‹ã§ ã€€ hoo f ã²ã¥ã‚ ã€€ shiver ã¶ã‚‹ã¶ã‚‹éœ‡ãˆã‚‹ ã€€ chil l å†·ãˆã‚‹ã€å¯’æ°—ã‚’æ„Ÿã˜ã‚‹ ã€€ flam e ç‚

 


Now` mid the hills the Momiji

Is trampled down `neath hoof of deer

Whose plaintive cries continually

Are heard both far and near;

My shivering frame

Now autumn`s piercing chills doth blame.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


In the mountain depths,

Treading through the crimson leaves,

Cries the wandering stag.

When I hear the lonely cry,

Sad,- how sad - the autumn is!

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

When the glitter of the hoarfrost is seen on the bridge of magpies, I feel that the break of day is near.

 

ã‹ã•ã•ãã®æ©‹ã®ä¸Šã«éœ§æ°·ã®ãã‚‰ã‚ããŒè¦‹ãˆã‚‹ã¨ãã€ç§ã¯å¤œæ˜ã‘ãŒè¿‘ã„ã¨æ„Ÿã˜ã‚‹ã€‚

Upon the bridge where ravens, aye,

Do love to pass where hoar-frost`s sheen.

When hoar-frost`s glittering film is seen;

I trow the break of the day is nigh.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If the "Magpie Bridge" -

Bridge by flight of magpies spanned,-

White with frost I see: -

With a deep-laid frost made white:-

Late, I know, has grown the night.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

On every side the vaulted sky

I view: now will the moon have peered,

I trow, above Mikasa high

In Kasuga`s far-off land upreared.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


When I look abroad

O`er the wide-stretched "Plain of Heaven,"

Is the moon the same

That on Mount Mikasa rose,

In the land of Kasuga?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

My cabin is in far southeast of the capital, where I enjoy the solitary life.  Men may call it Uji mountain yet I dwell here.

My cabin doth in Tats`mi lie

Miyako`s city near,

Yo-uji men my mountain call,

Yet still do I dwell here.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Lowly hut is mine
South-east from the capital: -
Thus I choose to dwell;-
And the world in which I live
Men have named a "Mount of Gloom."

 (Translated by MacCauley)

Your love has passed away from me and left me desolate.  I mourn how wearily the summer passed away in winter rains.

 

ã‚ãªãŸã®æ„›ãŒå»ã‚Šã€æ®‹ã•ã‚ŒãŸç§ã¯è¦‹ã‚‹å½±ã‚‚ãªãã‚ã³ã—ã„ã€‚å†¬ã®é›¨ã®ãªã‹ã€å¤ãŒç‰©æ†‚ãéãå»ã£ãŸã®ã‚’ç§ã¯å˜†ãæ‚²ã—ã‚€ã€‚

Thy love hath passed away from me,

Left desolate, forlorn,

In winter-rains how wearily,

Thy summer past I mourn!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Color of the flower

Has already passed away

While on trivial things

Vainly I have set my gaze,

In my journey through the world.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Some in haste to the city or from the city, here friends and strangers meet and part with kindly glance.  The name of Osaka gate which men have given is apt to you.

Some hence towards the city haste,

Some from the city here speed by,

Here friends and strangers meet and part,

With kindly glance and careless eye;

Apt is the name it seems to me,

Ausaka gate, men give to thee.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Truly, this is where

Travelers who go or come

Over parting ways,--

Friends or strangers,--all must meet;

`Tis the gate of "Meeting Hill."

(Translated by MacCauley)

You fishermen who range the boats on the coast, I pray you, tell my fellow villagers that how far I go over ocean`s smell.  I sail in a frail ship towards tens of islands.

Ye fishermen, who range the sea,

In many a barque, I pray ye tell

My fellow-villagers of me --

How that far o`er vast ocean`s swell.

In vessel frail,

Towards Yasoshima I sail.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


O`er the wide, wide sea,

Towards its many distant isles,

Rowing I set forth.

This, to all the world proclaim,

O ye boats of fisher-folk!

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

In fitful path across the sky a cloud-born maiden glides by the wind of heaven.  Now the vigor of breeze has lasted an instant and her bright form greets my sight for fleeting moment.

In fitful path across the sky,

By various winds of heaven forced,

Cloud-borne Otome glideth by --

Now hath the breeze its vigour lost

An instant, and her form so bright

For a fleeting moment greets my sight.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


O ye Winds of Heaven!

In the paths among the clouds

Blow, and close the ways,

That we may these virgin forms

Yet a little while detain.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The water of the Minanogawa falls from the lofty peak of Tsukubane mountain and accumulates to seek the end of all its constant flow to the sea that knows no limits.

 

 

ã¿ãªã®å·ã®æ°´ã¯ã€ã¤ãã°ã­å±±ã®é«˜ã„é ‚ä¸Šã‹ã‚‰è½ã¡ã¦æºœã¾ã‚Šã€ãã®ä¸€å®šã®æµã‚Œã®çµ‚ã‚ã‚Šã‚’æ¢ã£ã¦ã‹ã‚‰ã€é™ã‚Šãªã„æµ·ã¸ã¨æµã‚Œã¦ã„ãã€‚

The Minagawa`s waters fall

From Ts`kubane yama`s lofty peak:

In loving haste the waters all

For aye accumulate, and seek

The end of all their constant flow,

The sea that doth no limits know.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


From Tsukuba`s peak,

Falling waters have become

Mina`s still, full flow:

So my love has grown to be;

Like the river`s quiet deeps.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

My soul is vexed, crowded and perplexed which is more confused than varied patterns on Mochizuri fabric, the produce of Shinobu`s loom in Michinoku; for whose sake the gloom hold over my failing heart.

Ah me! My soul with cares is vext,

Unnumbered, crowded, and perplext,

Than varied pattern more confus`d,

On Mojidsuri fabric used,

The produce of Shinobu`s loom,

Shinobu in Michinolu land;

For whose sake whose but thine doth gloom

Hold o`er my failing heart command.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Michinoku print

Of shinobu`s tangled leaves!

For whose sake have I,

Like confused, begun to be?

Only yours! I cannot change!

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

For your sake I have stayed amid the meadows in this springtime and sought with care the young plant.  The snow that was left on it traced my sleeve.

Thy wishes, love, have I obeyed,

And` mid the meadows have I strayed

In this spring-time, and sought with care

The wakana plant that groweth there.

Lo on my sleeve

The falling snow its trace doth leave.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


It is for thy sake

That I seek the fields in spring,

Gathering green herbs,

While my garment`s hanging sleeves

Are with falling snow beflecked.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Inaba`s lofty range is crowned

By many a tall pine-tree;

Ah quickly were I homewards bound

If thou shouldst pine for me.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though we parted be;

If on Mount Inaba`s peak

I should hear the sound

Of the pine trees growing there,

Back at once I`ll make my way.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

O Tatsta! When th`autumnal flow

I watch of thy deep, ruddy wave --

E`en when the stern gods long ago

Did rule. Was ne`er beheld so brave,

So far the stream as thine, I vow.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


I have never heard

That, e`en when the gods held sway

In the ancient days,

E`er was water bound with red

Such as here in Tatta`s stream

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

On the shore of Sumi`s bay!

E`en in gathered night,

I must shun the eyes of men.

That grow Nanihagata nigh.

While time o`er e`en as brief space speed

I fain would die!

In this life? This, do you ask?

Distracted by my misery

How utterly forlorn am I;

Oh that might thee once more see,

Tho` it should cost my life to me!

Now, in dire distress,

It is all the same to me!

So, then, let us meet

Even though it costs my life

In the Bay of Naniwa.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Oh, maiden! heedless of thy vow, 
Why com`st thou not? ` Tis "long-moon" night, 
And th`Ariake moon shines now,

"In a moment I will come," 
I`ve awaited her 
E`en until the moon of dawn, 
In the long month, hath appeared.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Now autumn`s gales, in various freak

On herb, on tree, destruction wreak,

And wildest roar

The guts that down from Mube   Pour.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Since `tis by its breath

Autumn`s leaves of grass and trees

Riven are and waste,--

Men may to the mountain wind

Fitly given the name, "The Wild."

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

How oft` my glance upon the moon hath dwelt,

Her secret power my soul subdued --

Her sadd`ning influence I alone have felt,

Though all men autumn`s moon have viewed.

 

(Translated by Dickins)
 



Gaze I at the moon,
Myriad things arise in thought,
And my thoughts are sad;-
Yet, `tis not for me alone,
That the autumn time has come.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

If thou`rt as fair rumour thee

Doth paint` O deign my but to grace

And may thy path as secret be

To human eye as is the trace

Of Sanekads`ra`med

Osaka-yama`s forests hid!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If thy name be true,

Trailing vine of "Meeting Hill,"

Is there not some way

Whereby, without ken of men,

I can draw thee to my side?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

How pleasant to see the reddened leaves of the maple tree on Ogura mountain!  They would linger till there passed again our Emperor`s train.

The redd`ning leaf of th` Momiji 

that on Ogura`s summit grow, 

how pleasant `tis their tints to see!  

Ah!  Did thy but their beauty know, 

they would linger till there pass`d again 

our emperor`s Miyuki train.

 

(Translated by Dickins)

 


If the maple leaves

On Ogura mountain

Could only have hearts,

They would longingly await

The emperor`s pilgrimage .

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Lo Idsmi `s boiling waters flow,

With tumult vast, through Mika`s plain;

My mind doth like confusion know,

A wretched prey to lover`s pain.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Over Mika`s plain,

Gushing forth and flowing free,

Is Izumi`s stream.

I know not if we have met:

Why, then, do I long for her?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The lonely hamlet is amid the hill; I feel its solitude with misery in winter time, for now severe climate has taken away every herb and tree and even human face from me.

The hamlet bosom`d`mid the hills

Aye lonely is; in winter-time

Its solitude with mis`ry fills

My mind, for now the rig`rous clime

Hath banished every herb and tree

And every human face from me.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Winter loneliness

In a mountain hamlet grows

Only deeper, when

Guests are gone, and leaves and grass

Withered are; - so runs my thought.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I had to pluck thee, flower, -thought-

To pluck thee, flower, in vain I sought:

The earliest hoar-frost feigning thee,

Fair Shiragiku, cheated me.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If it were my wish

White chrysanthemum to cull;--

Puzzled by the frost

Of the early autumn time,

I by chance might pluck the flower.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The Ariake-moonbeams will

In th`morning heaven linger still;

While I from thee -- how hard the smart --

By Akatsuki compelled, must part.

 

 (Translated by Dickins)

 


Like the morning moon,

Cold, unpitying was my love.

Since that parting hour,

Nothing I dislike so much

As the breaking light of day

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Now clearly broke the dawning day,

Ariaka moon I thought to see --

The new-fallen snow that lay

Round Yoshino deceived me.

The whitened hillside seemed

As tho` thereon the moonlight streamed.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


At the break of day,

Just as though the morning moon

Lightened the dim scene,

Yoshino`s fair hamlet lay

In a haze of falling snow.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The winds of autumn have amassed

Dried withered leaves in ruddy heaps,

Have them in th`mountain torrent cast,

Whose stream in stony channel sweeps;

Amid the rocks that bar the way

The Momiji`s reddened leaves delay.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


In a mountain stream,

Builded by the busy wind,

Is a wattled-barrier drawn.

Yet `tis only maple leaves

Powerless to flow away.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

It is a pleasant day of merry Spring and there are no bitter frosts, no storm winds, no rain clouds, and the sun shines brightly on high, but you poor trembling little flower, why do you wither away and die?

`Tis pleasant day of merry spring;

No bitter frosts are threatening,

No storm-winds blow, no rain-clouds low`r,

The sun shines bright on high,

Yet thou, poor trembling little flow`r,

Dost wither away and die.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


In the cheerful light

Of the ever-shining Sun,

In the days of spring;

Why, with ceaseless, restless haste

Falls the cherry`s new-blown bloom?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

There are no old companions left but the ancient pine trees that grow on the Takasago`s peak.  Comrades of many years have now all gone, and no friends for whom I mourn.

 

 

é«˜ç ‚ã®å±±é ‚ã«ç”Ÿãˆã¦ã„ã‚‹æ¾ã®æœ¨ã€…ã®ã»ã‹ã«ã¯ã€æ—§ã„å‹ã¯ã„ãªã„ã€‚é•·å¹´ã®ä»²é–“ã¯ã™ã¹ã¦å»ã‚Šï¼ˆé€ãï¼‰ã€ç§ã«ã¯å˜†ãæ‚²ã—ã‚€ã¹ãå‹ã‚‚ãªã„ã€‚

èªå¥ ã€€ companio n ä»²é–“ ã€€ bu t ã‚’é™¤ã„ã¦ã€ä»¥å¤–ã¯ ã€€ ancien t æ˜”ã® ã€€ pine tre e æ¾ã®æœ¨ ã€€ comrad e ä»²é–“ã€åŒå¿— ã€€ mour n å˜†ãæ‚²ã—ã‚€

 


Of old companions bereft,

Men`s friendship more I may not seek,

Nought but the ancient prine-trees left,

That`s grow on Takasago`s peak,

Comrades of many a year no gone,

But not the friends for whom I mourn.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Whom then are there now,

In my age (so far advanced)

I can hold as friends?

Even Takasago`s pines

Are not friends of former days.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The comrades of my early days, with the wandering eyes, gaze on me with an indifferent view.  O flower!  Your fragrance alone seems to me familiar in this old village I know so well.

 

 

æ˜”ã®ä»²é–“ãŸã¡ã¯ã€ã•ã¾ã‚ˆã†ã‚ˆã†ãªç›®ã¤ãã§ã€ç§ã«ç„¡é–¢å¿ƒãªã¾ãªã–ã—ã‚’å‘ã‘ãŸã€‚ã‚ã‚ã€èŠ±ã‚ˆã€ã“ã®ã‚ˆãçŸ¥ã£ãŸæ—§ã„æ‘ã§ã€ç§ã«ã¯ã€ã‚ãªãŸã®é¦™ã‚Šã ã‘ãŒé¦´æŸ“ã¿æ·±ã„ã‚‚ã®ã«æ€ãˆã‚‹ã€‚

The comrades of my early days

Their former friend in different view,

Who with a wondering eye doth gaze

On th`village that of he knew

So well. O flower! Thy fragrancy

Alone familiar seems to me.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


No! no! As for man,

How his heart is none can tell,

But the plum`s sweet flower

In my birthplace, as of yore,

Still emits the same perfume.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

It was a summer night and I scarcely thought the evening hour had passed away when dawn broke.  I longed for the moon that I sought or I did not know where she lay amid the cloud.

 

 

å¤ã®å¤œã§ã‚ã£ãŸã®ã§ã€å¤œãŒæ˜ã‘ãŸã¨ãã€ç§ã¯å®µã®æ™‚é–“ãŒã»ã¨ã‚“ã©éãå»ã£ã¦ã„ãªã„ã‚ˆã†ãªæ°—ãŒã—ãŸã€‚ç§ã¯æ¢ã—ã¦ã„ãŸæœˆã‚’æ±‚ã‚ãŸãŒã€ãã†ã—ãªãã¦ã¯ã€æœˆãŒé›²ã®ã©ã“ã«ã„ã‚‹ã®ã‹ã€ã‚ã‹ã‚‰ãªã‹ã£ãŸã€‚

`Twas a summer`s night, I scarcely thought

The evening hours had passed away

When dawn broke; long the moon I`d sought,

Nor knew where`mid the clouds she lay.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


In the summer night,

While the evening still seems here,

Lo! the dawn has come.

In what region of the clouds

Has the wandering moon found place?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

New sparkling dewdrops are seen over the moor, and the autumn gust sweeps howling by.  The dewdrops fly in a timid shower and there they lie scattered over the grass.

Now dew-drops sparkling o`er the moor are seen,

The autumn guts sweeps howling by,

Scarce lurks an instant`mid the reeds I ween:

In timid show`r the dew-drops fly,

And, scattered o`er the grass, there lie.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


In the autumn fields,

When the heedless wind blows by

O`er the pure-white dew,

How the myriad unstrung gems

Everywhere are scattered round!

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

èªå¥ ã€€ oat h èª“ã„ ã€€ swor n èª“ã£ãŸ ï¼ˆ swea r ã®éå»å½¢ï¼‰ã€€ã€€scor n è»½è”‘ã™ã‚‹ã€çªã£ã±ã­ã‚‹ ã€€ penalt y ç½°

 

 


A solemn oath thou swor`st with me,

I dreamt thou wouldest constant be --

Forgotten, scorned, the penalty

Of death I almost cry on thee.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though forgotten now,

For myself I do not care:

He, by oath, was pledged;-

And his life, who is forsworn,

That is, ah! so pitiful.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I wished to conceal my passion as if the reeds of Ono moor were hidden, but my secret passion and my ardent love were revealed as by their flower.

Like humble Asajiu amid

The reeds of Ono`s moor hid,

I would my passion were concealed

But by its flower the Asajiu:

By my too ardent love for you

My secret passion stands revealed.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Bamboo-growing plain,

With a small-field bearing reeds!

Though I bear my lot,

Why is it too much to bear?

Why do I still love her so?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Though I tried to hide my love, yet my face tells the secret and my changing visage shows the deep passion that dwells in me.  All men ask what grief has altered my feature.

Tho`aye I strive my lot to hide,

My face to all the secret tells:

My changing visage, sorely tried,

Shows that deep passion in me dwells:

And all men ask,

What griefs my altered features task?

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though I would conceal,

In my face it yet appears,-

My fond, secret love:-

So much that he asks of me,

"Does not something trouble you?"

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

My love for thee of every tongue

The daily theme is -- far and wide

My name is bruited men among.

Ah me! My heart was sorely tried

With no unfounded fears, lest

My love to all should stand confest.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though, indeed, I love,

Yet, the rumor of my love

Had gone far and wide,

When no man, ere then, could know

That I had begun to love.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

When we embraced each other and swore a solemn vow of faith, we sealed it with the tears we shed.  I drenched my cheek as our oath would fail to keep when the waves leapt over the pine-crowned steep of Suenomatsuyama.

èªå¥ ã€€ embrac e æŠ±ãã—ã‚ã‚‹ ã€€ swea r èª“ã† ã€€ solemn ã¾ã˜ã‚ãªã€å³ç²›ãª ã€€ vo w èª“ã„ ã€€ fait h ä¿¡å¿µã€èª å®Ÿ ã€€ sea l å°å°ã™ã‚‹ ã€€ she d ï¼ˆæ¶™ã‚’ï¼‰æµã™ ã€€ drenc h ãšã¶æ¿¡ã‚Œã«ã™ã‚‹ ã€€ oat h èª“ã„ ã€€ leap ove r è·³ã³è¶Šãˆã‚‹ ã€€ pine-crowne d æ¾ã®æœ¨ã§é ‚ä¸ŠãŒè¦†ã‚ã‚ŒãŸ ã€€ stee p çµ¶å£


When last each other we embraced,
A solemn vow of faith we swore,
And sealed it with the tears that chased
Down my cheeks our drench`d sleeves o`er --
That we our oath would fail to keep
When th`waves o`erleapt S`ye`s pine-crown`d steep.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Have we not been pledged 
By the wringing of our sleeves,-- 
Each for each in turn,-- 
That o`er Sue`s Mount of Pines 
Ocean waves shall never pass?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I went to see you, my dearest maid, and when I parted from you, I mourned such misery on my soul that I have never experienced, before I have seen you.

 

 

æ„›ã™ã‚‹ä¹™å¥³ã‚ˆã€ç§ãŒã‚ãªãŸã«ä¼šã„ã«è¡Œãã€ãã—ã¦ã‚ãªãŸã¨åˆ¥ã‚ŒãŸã¨ãã€ç§ã¯ã€ã‚ãªãŸã¨ä¼šã†å‰ã«ã¯æ±ºã—ã¦çµŒé¨“ã—ãŸã“ã¨ã®ãªã„å¿ƒã®è‹¦ã—ã¿ã‚’å˜†ãæ‚²ã—ã‚“ã ã€‚

I went to meet thee, dearest maid,

And when I parted loth from thee,

Upon my soul such mis`ry weighed,

I mourned the love that burdened me:

O that my heart

Were still unvexed by lover`s smart!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Having met my love,

Afterwards my passion was,

When I measured it

With the feeling of the past,

As, if then, I had not loved.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

If loving is not a human fate, then men would not hate themselves or their fellows, since the passion of love has ever born in them.

 

 

ã‚‚ã—æ„›ã™ã‚‹ã“ã¨ãŒäººé–“ã®å®¿å‘½ã§ãªã„ã®ãªã‚‰ã€æ„›ã®æƒ…ç†±ãŒè‡ªã‚‰ã®ä¸­ã«ç”Ÿã¾ã‚ŒãŸã‹ã‚‰ã¨ã„ã£ã¦ã€äººã¯è‡ªåˆ†ã‚„ä»²é–“ã‚’æ†ã‚€ã“ã¨ã‚‚ãªã„ã ã‚ã†ã«ã€‚

To love, were it not human fate,

Then men their fellows would not shun,

Their very selves they would not hate,

As since love`s birth they`ve ever done.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If a trysting time

There should never be at all,

I should not complain

For myself (oft left forlorn),

Or of her (in heartless mood).

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

You cruel one!  You passed by me yet you turned no glance of pity on me, but a careless scorn was in your eyes and you mocked the passion that was burning in me.

 

 

æ®‹é…·ãªã‚ãªãŸã€‚ã‚ãªãŸã¯ç§ã®ãã°ã‚’é€šã‚Šéãã¦ã€ç§ã«ã‚ã‚ã‚Œã¿ã®ã¾ãªã–ã—ã‚’å‘ã‘ã‚‹ã“ã¨ã‚‚ãªã‹ã£ãŸã®ã«ã€ã‚ãªãŸã®ç›®ã«ã¯ä¸æ³¨æ„ãªè»½è”‘ãŒã‚ã‚Šã€ç§ã®ä¸­ã«ç‡ƒãˆã¦ã„ã‚‹æƒ…ç†±ã‚’ã‚ã–ã‘ã£ã¦ã„ãŸã®ã§ã™ã€‚

Ah, cruel one! thou pass`d me by

No glance of pity on turned,

A careless scorn was in thine eye,

That mock`d the passion that in me burn`d:

Alas! Alas!

Such woes my failing pow`rs surpass.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Sure that there is none

Who will speak a pitying word,

I shall pass away.

Ah! my death shall only be

My own folly`s (fitting end).

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The fisher`s barks glide over the broad expanse of Yura bay safely, but if their rudder is lost over the tide they stray uncertain path in vain.  The course of love too pursues uncertain path like fishers` boats.

 

 

æ¼å¸«ã®èˆ¹ã¯ç”±è‰¯æ¹¾ã®åºƒå¤§ãªæµ·åŸã‚’å®‰å…¨ã«ã™ã¹ã‚‹ã‚ˆã†ã«é€²ã‚€ãŒã€ã‚‚ã—æ½®ã®æµã‚Œã«èˆµã‚’å¤±ãã—ã¦ã—ã¾ã£ãŸã‚‰ã€ä¸ç¢ºã‹ãªè·¯ã‚’ã‚€ãªã—ãã•ã¾ã‚ˆã†ã“ã¨ã«ãªã‚‹ã€‚æ‹ã®è·¯ã‚‚ã¾ãŸã€æ¼å¸«ã®èˆŸã®ã‚ˆã†ã«ä¸ç¢ºã‹ãªè·¯ã‚’é€²ã‚“ã§ã„ãã‚‚ã®ã ã€‚

The fisher`s barque in safety glide

O`er th`board expanse of Yura bay,

Their rudder lot o`ter Yura`s tide

In vague uncertain path they stray:

The course of love doth, too,

A like uncertain path persue.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Like a mariner

Sailing over Yura`s strait

With his rudder gone,--

Whither, o`er the deep of love,

Lies the goal, I do not know.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Vines are grown over my mountain dwelling`s roof and I live alone and uncheered without catching any glimpse of passers-by, yet autumn has come.

 

 

ç§ã®å±±ã®ä½ã¾ã„ã®å±‹æ ¹ã«ã¯ã¤ã‚‹è‰ãŒèŒ‚ã£ã¦ã„ã¦ã€ç§ã¯é€šè¡Œäººã®ç›®ã«è§¦ã‚Œã‚‹ã“ã¨ã‚‚ãªãä¸€äººã‚ã³ã—ãæš®ã‚‰ã—ã¦ã„ã‚‹ãŒã€ãã‚Œã§ã‚‚ç§‹ã¯ã‚„ã£ã¦ããŸã€‚

My mountain dwelling`s roof of thatch

Is with Yahemugura moss o`ergrown,

Of passer-by no glimpse I catch,

I dwell uncheered and alone:

`Tis autumn time,

And mankind dread the rig`rous clime.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


To the humble cot,

Overgrown with thick-leaved vines

In its loneliness,

Comes the dreary autumn time;--

And not even man is there.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The wind is flung backward from the pitiless rock in scattered spray.  With prayers from my heart in anguish I seek to make you love and obey.  Do the rocks and the waves mock my passion?

From th` pitiless rock are backwards flung

The wind urged floods in scattered spray.

With prayers from anguished heart-depth wrung,

I seek to make thee love`obey;

As spurns the rock

The waves, dost thou my passion mock.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Like a driven wave,

Dashed by fierce winds on a rock,

So it is, alas!

Crushed and all alone am I;

Thinking over what has been.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The watchmen keep fire blazing brightly all through night, but when morning breaks they extinguish the flame.  So too at dawn, my passion is passed and gone.

 

 

å¤œè­¦ã¯å¤œé€šã—ç«ã‚’æ˜ã‚‹ãç‡ƒãˆç«‹ãŸã›ã‚‹ãŒã€å¤œãŒæ˜ã‘ã‚‹ã¨ç‚ã‚’æ¶ˆã™ã€‚ã ã‹ã‚‰ç§ã‚‚å¤œæ˜ã‘ã«ãªã‚‹ã¨ã™ãã«ã€ç†±ã„æ€ã„ãŒéãå»ã£ã¦æ¶ˆãˆã¦ã—ã¾ã†ã€‚

Th`Mikakimori through the night

(And men the warder Yeji name)

The watch-fire`s blaze keeps full and bright;

When morning breaks, then dies the flame:

So, too, at dawn

My happiness is past and gone.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Like the warder`s fires

At the Imperial gateway kept,--

Burning through the night,

Through the day in ashes dulled,--

Is the love aglow in me.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Before I worshiped you, my dear maid, I had not cared for my life, but now I wish to be granted with long years that I might live with you.

Ere I, O maid! Had worshipped thee,

A drear, uncared-for life was mine: -

O may long years be granted me,

Now that my heart, O maid, is thine!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


For thy precious sake,

Once my (eager) life itself

Was not dear to me.

But `tis now my heart`s desire

It may long, long years endure.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I cannot tell you my burning love for you, which is comparable to the burning moxa on the Ibuki mountain.  You might not know, but I hope you would sympathize my ardent passion that is so intense within me.

 

 

ç§ã¯ã€ã„ã¶ãå±±ã®ç‡ƒãˆã‚‹ã‚‚ãã•ã«ã‚‚å–©ãˆã‚‰ã‚Œã‚‹ã€ã‚ãªãŸã¸ã®ç‡ƒãˆã‚‹æ‹ã‚’ã€ã‚ãªãŸã«è¨€ã†ã“ã¨ãŒã§ãã¾ã›ã‚“ã€‚ã‚ãªãŸã¯ã”å­˜ã˜ãªã„ã§ã—ã‚‡ã†ãŒã€ç§ã®ä¸­ã«ã‚ã‚‹ã“ã‚“ãªã«ã‚‚å¼·çƒˆãªã€ç§ã®ç†±ã„æƒ…ç†±ã«ã‚ãªãŸãŒåŒæƒ…ã—ã¦ãã‚ŒãŸã‚‰ã„ã„ã®ã«ã€‚

To tell thee of my love were vain,

Its depth to me is scarcely known:

As writhes the flesh `neath Moxa`s pain,

The Moxa on Ibuki grown,

So madly writhes my spirit`mong

Love`s flames, ere now unknown, sore wrung.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


That, `tis as it is,

How can I make known to her?

So, she may n`er know

That the love I feel for her

Like Ibuki`s moxa burns.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Though I fully know that the darkness of ensuing night will come, yet when day breaks, I hate the daylight and bitterly mourn the unwelcome breaking of the dawn.

 

 

å¾Œã«ç¶šãå¤œã®é—‡ãŒã¾ãŸã‚„ã£ã¦æ¥ã‚‹ã“ã¨ã¯ååˆ†ã«çŸ¥ã£ã¦ã„ã‚‹ã‚‚ã®ã®ã€ãã‚Œã§ã‚‚ä¸–ãŒæ˜ã‘ã‚‹ã¨ã€ç§ã¯æ—¥ã®å…‰ã‚’æ¨ã‚ã—ãæ€ã„ã€æ­“è¿ã•ã‚Œãªã„å¤œæ˜ã‘ã‚’è‹¦ã€…ã—ãå˜†ãæ‚²ã—ã‚€ã®ã§ã™ã€‚

When day breaks, tho`full well I know

The darkness of the ensuing night

The hated day shall overthrow:

Yet aye the daylight do I hate,

And bitterly mourn

Th`unwelcome breaking of the dawn.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though I know full well

That the night will come again

E`en when day has dawned,

Yet, in truth, I hate the sight

Of the morning`s coming light.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I have watched weeping through the night,

Deserted, desolate, alone,

Till now hath broke the morning light

I almost deemed for ever gone,

So slowly by

The creeping hours seemed to hie.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Sighing all alone,

Through the long watch of the night,

Till the break of day:--

Can you realize at all

What a tedious thing it is?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Keeping the vows that lovers swear of faithfulness and constancy through life till death is the overtask for human frailty.  I would rather die if I could, while we are yet in ardent love.

To keep the vows that lovers swear

Of faithfulness and constancy

Through life till death and worldly care,

O`ertasketh human frailty,

I trow. To-day

I`d fain my spirit fled away.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If "not to forget"

Will for him in future years

Be too difficult;--

It were well this very day

That my life, ah me! should close.

 

(Mac Cauuley)

The noisy play of the waterfall

Hath ceased long ago,

Yet aye shall men its fame recall,

Tho` none now list its flow.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though the waterfall

In its flow ceased long ago,

And its sound is stilled;

Yet, in name it ever flows,

And in fame may yet be heard.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

My mind foretells me that this world shall end before long and before my soul shall go to the other world, but once more I wish to see you.

 

 

ã¾ã‚‚ãªãã“ã®ä¸–ç•ŒãŒçµ‚ã‚ã‚‹ã ã‚ã†ã¨ç§ã®å¿ƒãŒäºˆè¨€ã—ã¦ã„ã¾ã™ãŒã€ç§ã®é­‚ãŒåˆ¥ã®ä¸–ç•Œã¸è¡Œãå‰ã«ã€ã‚‚ã†ã„ã¡ã©ã‚ãªãŸã«ãŠä¼šã„ã—ãŸã„ã‚‚ã®ã§ã™ã€‚

Ere long for me this world shall end,

Thus doth my mind to me foretell;

Ere long to other world shall wend

My soul that thee hath lov`d so well.

Ah, would that thou

But once more wer`t beside me now.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Soon I cease to be;--

One fond memory I would keep

When beyond this world.

Is there, then, no way for me

Just once more to meet with thee?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I ventured forth one moonlight night,

And then saw some one hastening past,

Ere I could tell who` twas aright,

With dark clouds was the moon o`ercast,

Whose pallid ray

O`er th`middle night held tranquil sway.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Meeting in the way -,

While I can not clearly know

If `tis friend or not; -

Lo! the midnight moon, ah me!

In a cloud has disappeared.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Make fickle thou than th`winds that pour

Down Arima o`er Ina`s moor,

I have forgotten to forget.

If Mount Arima

Sends his rustling winds across

Ina`s bamboo-plains;--

Well! in truth, tis as you say;

Yet how can I e`er forget?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I wait thy coming, love - repose

Veils not mine eyes -- far in the night

I watch the moon till nigh the close.

Of her celestial path of light.

Care-free, than to keep vain watch

Through the passing night,

Till I saw the lonely moon.

Traverse her descending path.

The road that crosses o`er the plain

Towards Ikuno`s full long for thee,

The road that far away doth gain

The distant range of Ohoye:

Is so very far, -

The Yaye cherry flower, which has flourished at Nara, the old capital, is presented to the Imperial palace today, and its fragrance and scent spread all over.

Of old Yahezak`ra lent

To Nara, capital of yore,

Its fragrancy, and now its scent

Hath spread over our Kononohe o`er.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Eight-fold cherry flowers

That at Nara,--ancient seat

Of Our State,--have bloomed;--

In Our Nine-fold Palace court

Shed their sweet perfume today

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Though you might cheat the guards of Kankoku-kan`s gate with false cockcrow and thus anticipate them that the morning has come, yet you cannot cheat me and Osaka gate shall be kept till morning.

 

 

ã‚ãªãŸãªã‚‰å½ã®é¶ã®å£°ã§å‡½è°·é–¢ã®ç•ªäººã‚’ã ã¾ã—ã¦æœãŒæ¥ãŸã¨æ€ã‚ã›ã‚‹ã“ã¨ãŒã§ãã‚‹ã‹ã‚‚çŸ¥ã‚Œã¾ã›ã‚“ãŒã€ç§ã‚’ã ã¾ã™ã“ã¨ã¯ã§ããªã„ã—ã€é€¢å‚ã®é–¢ã¯æœã¾ã§é–‰ã¾ã£ã¦ã„ã‚‹ã§ã—ã‚‡ã†ã€‚

Tho` thou, the guardians of the gate

Of Kan-Koku`-Kan, with false cock-crow,

Might(st cheat, and thus anticipate

The morn, thou ne`er canst cheat, I trow,

Ausaka`s gate, that thee

Small keep until the morning be.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though in middle night,

By the feigned crow of the cock,

Some may be deceived;--

Yet, at Ausaka`s gate

This can never be achieved

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Now, deep misery oppresses my vexed heart and I can confess my sorrowful mind to none except you.  I wish to speak to you about all my wretchedness.

Now doth deep misery oppress

My vex`d and sorrow`d mind

To none will I my woe confess,

Save thee, among mankind:

With thee I seek

Of all my wretchedness to speak.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Is there now no way,

But through others` lips, to say

These so fateful words,--

That, henceforth, my love for you

I must banish from my thoughts?

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

In the dim gray light of early dawn I strayed by the Uji river where the morning fog flowed on its surface.  There were scattered glimpses of stakes whereon fishermen spread their net.

 

 

æ˜ã‘æ–¹ã®ã»ã®æš—ã„ç°è‰²ã®å…‰ã®ä¸­ã§ã€ç§ã¯å®‡æ²»å·ã®ã»ã¨ã‚Šã‚’ã•ã¾ã‚ˆã£ã¦ã„ãŸãŒã€ãã®å·ã®é¢ã«ã¯æœã®éœ§ãŒæµã‚Œã¦ã„ãŸã€‚æ¼å¸«ãŒç¶²ã‚’åºƒã’ã‚‹ãŸã‚ã®æ­ãŒã¡ã‚‰ã¡ã‚‰ã¨è¦‹ãˆã¦ã„ãŸã€‚

By th` dim grey light of early dawn

I stray`d by Uji`s wave,

From whence the rifting mist upborne

Me scattered glimpses gave.

Of Zeze`s stakes there set,

Whereon the fisher spreads his net.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Lo! at early dawn,

When the mists o`er Uji`s stream

Slowly lift and clear,

And the net-stakes on the shoals,

Near and far away, appear!

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

For your long negligence I weep and my tear drenches my sleeves.  The happiness of my life has all gone now, and all men despise my name that became the victim of love.

 

 

ã‚ãªãŸã«é•·ã„é–“ç„¡è¦–ã•ã‚ŒãŸã®ã§ç§ã¯æ³£ã„ã¦ã€æ¶™ãŒè¢–ã‚’ã²ã©ãæ¿¡ã‚‰ã—ã¾ã™ã€‚ã‚‚ã†ç§ã®å¹¸ã›ã¯ã™ã¹ã¦éãå»ã£ã¦ã€äººã€…ã¯ã¿ã‚“ãªæ„›ã®çŠ ç‰²ã¨ãªã£ãŸç§ã®åã‚’è»½è”‘ã™ã‚‹ã®ã§ã™ã€‚

Despised, I weep thy long neglect.

My tears drench my sleeves,

The happiness of my life is wrecked

In struggles to achieve

Thy stubborn love:

My fate might all men`s pity move.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Even when my sleeves,

Through my hate and misery,

Never once are dry,--

For such love my name decays:--

How deplorable my lot!

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

O mountain cherry tree!  I moan the lonely fate with you.  Your blossom only cheers me -- you are only my friend.

With thee, O mountain Sakura tree!

A lonely fate I moan.

Thy blossom only cheers me --

The only friend I own.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Let us, each for each

Pitying, hold tender thought,

Mountain-cherry flower!

Other than thee, lonely flower,

There is none I know as friend.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

If you proffered your arm as a pillow for my head at a charming spring-night -- dream that does not last long.  What will the rumour say?  How my fame would suffer from slandering blame!

 

 

ã‚‚ã—ã‚ãªãŸãŒç´ æ•µãªæ˜¥ã®å¤œã«ã€è…•ã‚’å·®ã—å‡ºã—ã¦ç§ã®æ•ã«ã—ã¦ãã‚Œã¦ã‚‚ã€å¤¢ã¯é•·ãç¶šãã¾ã›ã‚“ã€‚å™‚ã¯ä½•ã¨è¨€ã†ã§ã—ã‚‡ã†ã€‚ç§ã®åèª‰ã¯ã€éé›£ã«ã‚ˆã£ã¦ã©ã‚“ãªã«å‚·ã¤ãã§ã—ã‚‡ã†ã‹ã€‚

Had I made of thy proffer`d arm

A pillow for my wearied head,

No longer e`en than lasts the charm

Of a spring-night dream - what had rumour said?

How would my fame

Have suffer`d from men`s sland`ring blame!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If, but through the dreams

Of a spring`s short night, I`d rest

Pillowed on this arm,

And my name were blameless stained,

Hard, indeed, would be my fate.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I would no longer live in this so hard world with joy, but still I must live.  How wistfully my eyes regard the midnight moonbeam`s tranquil sway!

 

 

ã“ã®è¾›ã„ä¸–ã®ä¸­ã§ç§ã¯ã‚‚ã¯ã‚„å–œã³ã‚’ã‚‚ã£ã¦ç”Ÿãã‚‹ã“ã¨ã¯ãªã„ã ã‚ã†ãŒã€ãã‚Œã§ã‚‚ç§ã¯ç”Ÿããªãã¦ã¯ãªã‚‰ãªã„ã€‚çœŸå¤œä¸­ã®æœˆå…‰ãŒãŠã ã‚„ã‹ã«æºã‚Œã‚‹ã•ã¾ã‚’ã€ç§ã®ç›®ãŒä½•ã¨ã†ã‚‰ã‚„ã¾ã—ã’ã«çœºã‚ã‚‹ã“ã¨ã ã‚ã†ã‹ã€‚

Fain would I in this world so hard

No longer live, but still must stay:

How wistfully my eye regard

The midnight moonbeams` tranquil sway!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If, against my wish,

In the world of sorrows still,

I for long should live;--

How then I would pine, alas!

For this moon of middle-night.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The stormy winds roar and whirl lustily round the Mimuro mountain and scattered leaves of maple, reddening the stream of the Tatsuta river.

 

 

ä¸‰å®¤å±±ã®ã‚ãŸã‚Šã§ã¯åµãŒæ¿€ã—ãè½ŸéŸ³ã‚’ç«‹ã¦ã¦æ¸¦å·»ãã€ã‚‚ã¿ã˜ã®è‘‰ã‚’æ•£ã‚‰ã›ã¦ã€ç«œç”°å·ã‚’èµ¤ãè‰²ã¥ã‹ã›ã‚‹ã€‚

Round Mimuro-yama lustily

The storm-winds roar and whirl,

And th`scatter`d leaves of th`momiji

In the reddening Tatsuta hurl.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


By the wind-storm`s blast,

From Mimuro`s mountain slopes

Maples leaves are torn,

And as (rich) brocades, are wrought

On (blue) Tatta`s (quiet) stream.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

When I stay out from my cabin in solitude and wherever my wandering eyes may roam, all landscape appears desolate and dreadful in autumn.

 

 

ç§‹ã«ã€ã²ã¨ã‚Šå¯‚ã—ãå°å±‹ã‚’å‡ºã¦å¤–ã«ã„ã‚‹ã¨ã€ç§ã®ç›®ãŒã©ã“ã‚’ã•ã¾ã‚ˆã£ãŸã¨ã—ã¦ã‚‚ã€ï¼ˆç›®ã«ã™ã‚‹ï¼‰é¢¨æ™¯ã¯ã™ã¹ã¦ã‚ã³ã—ãã€ãŠãã‚ã—ã’ã«è¦‹ãˆã‚‹ã€‚

 

 

èªå¥ ã€€ cabi n å°å±‹ ã€€ solitud e å­¤ç‹¬ ã€€ whereve r ã©ã“ã§ã‚‚ãƒ»ãƒ»ãƒ»ã®ã¨ã“ã‚ã«ã€ã¨ã—ã¦ã‚‚ ã€€ wande r ã•ã¾ã‚ˆã†ã€ãµã‚‰ã¤ã ã€€ roa m ã¶ã‚‰ã¤ã ã€€ landscap e é¢¨æ™¯ ã€€ appea r ãƒ»ãƒ»ãƒ»ã®ã‚ˆã†ã«è¦‹ãˆã‚‹ ã€€ desolat e é¡§ã¿ã‚‰ã‚Œãªã„ã€ã‚ã³ã—ã„ ã€€ dreadfu l ãŠãã‚ã—ã„ã€ã‚‚ã®ã™ã”ã„


In lonely solitude my home

And from my cabin when I stray,

Where`er my wande`ring eyes may roam,

The landscape that doth round me lay,

How desolate, how drear

Doth it at autumn e`en appear.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


In my loneliness

From my humble home gone forth,

When I looked around,

Everywhere it was the same; -

One lone, darkening autumn eve.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Now, twilight darkens and the wind rustles in the rice field by the home.  The sweeping gusts of autumn pass my hut thatched with reed.

 

 

ä»Šã€ãŸããŒã‚Œã®æš—ãŒã‚ŠãŒã¾ã—ã¦å®¶ã®ãã°ã®ç”°ã‚“ã¼ã§é¢¨ãŒã‹ã•ã‹ã•ã¨éŸ³ã‚’ç«‹ã¦ã‚‹ã€‚ç§‹ã®æŠ¼ã—æµã™ã‚ˆã†ãªçªé¢¨ãŒã€è‘¦ã§å±‹æ ¹ã‚’è‘ºã„ãŸç§ã®æ±å±‹ã‚’é€šã‚Šéãã¦ã„ãã€‚

Now twilight darkens, and the breeze

Rustles the homeside rice-fields`mong,

And murmuring sounds my ear please,

As past my hut with thatch o`erhung of Ashi grass,

The sweeping gusts of autumn pass.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


When the evening comes,

From the rice leaves at my gate

Gentle knocks are heard;

And, into my round rush-hut,

Autumn`s roaming breeze makes way.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Your beauty is known through the land like the billows on Takashi`s shore, which drench the venturesome who comes too near.  So she who once meets you will shed the tears of jealous love, and her sleeve shall never be dried.

 

 

ã‚ãªãŸã®ç¾ã—ã•ã¯ã€å¤§èƒ†ã«ã‚‚è¿‘ä»˜ãäººã‚’ãšã¶æ¿¡ã‚Œã«ã—ã¦ã—ã¾ã†ã¨ã„ã†é«˜å¸«ã®æµ·å²¸ã®å¤§æ³¢ã®ã‚ˆã†ã«å›½ä¸­ã«çŸ¥ã‚‰ã‚Œã¦ã„ã¾ã™ã€‚ã§ã™ã‹ã‚‰ã€ã‚ãªãŸã«ä¸€åº¦ä¼šã£ãŸå¥³æ€§ã¯ã€æ„›ã®å«‰å¦¬ã®æ¶™ã‚’æµã—ã€å½¼å¥³ã®è¢–ãŒä¹¾ãã“ã¨ã¯æ±ºã—ã¦ãªã„ã§ã—ã‚‡ã†ã€‚

The beauty is throughout the land

As well-known as the furious play

Of billows on Takashi`s strand,

That drench the venturesome with spray,

Who come their sweep to nigh:

So she who hath thee once beheld,

To tears of jealous love compelled,

Her sleeve shall ne`er be dry.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Well I know the fame

Of the fickle waves that beat

On Takashi`s strand!

Should I e`er go near that shore

I should only wet my sleeves.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The cherry trees grow in plenty on Takasago`s steep hillside and show their crowded blossoms.  I hope no fog will hide their beauty and no mist will veil their radiance from our eyes.

 

 

é«˜ç ‚ã®é™ºã—ã„å±±è…¹ã«æ¡œã®æœ¨ã€…ãŒè±Šã‹ã«æˆé•·ã—ã€ã‚ãµã‚Œã‚“ã°ã‹ã‚Šã®èŠ±ã‚’è¦‹ã›ã¦ã„ã‚‹ã€‚éœ§ãŒãã®ç¾ã—ã•ã‚’éš ã—ãŸã‚Šã€éœãŒãã®è¼ãã‚’ç§ãŸã¡ã®ç›®ã‹ã‚‰è¦†ã£ãŸã‚Šã—ãªã‘ã‚Œã°ã„ã„ã®ã ãŒã€‚

The Sakura trees in plenty grow

On Takasago`s steep hill-side,

And now their crowded blossoms show;

O may no fogs their beauty hide,

No mists from hill-top rise,

To veil their radiance from our eyes.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


On that distant mount,

O`er the slope below the peak,

Cherries are in flower;--

May the mists of hither hills

Not arise to veil the scene

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Like the windy blasts sweeping down from the Hatsuse`s steep, you scorn your lover`s helpless plight.  I will pray no more before Hatsuse`s shrine as I rather doubt its fulfillment.

As windy blasts down Hasse`s steep

In furious path impetuous sweep,

So rudely thou my suit dost slight,

And scorn thy lover`s hapless plight;

No more` fore Hasse`s shrine

Will I ensuing prayer incline.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


I did not make prayer

(At the shrine of Mercy`s God),

That the unkind one

Should become as pitiless

As the storms of Hase`s hills.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The covenant that you made with me is my very life.  Alas!  I fear this autumn days are now but short.

A covenant thou mad`st with me,

And as the Sasemo from th`dew,

So I my very life from thee

Drink it. Alas! I fear me.

This autumn`s days are now but few!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though your promise was

"Like the dew on moxa plant"

And, to me, was life;

Yet, alas! the year has passed

Even into autumn time.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

In a fisher`s boat I glide over the broad expanse of ocean toward the horizon.  When I turn my glance, I can scarcely discern white-tipped billows with the cloud-horizon blend.

èªå¥ ã€€ glid e æ»‘ã‚‹ã‚ˆã†ã«è¡Œã ã€€ broa d åºƒã„ ã€€ expans e ï¼ˆç©ºãƒ»æµ·ãƒ»é™¸ãªã©ã®ï¼‰åºƒãŒã‚Š ã€€ horizo n æ°´å¹³ç·šãƒ»åœ°å¹³ç·š ã€€ glanc e ä¸€ç›®ã€ä¸€ç¥ ã€€ scarcel y ã»ã¨ã‚“ã©ãƒ»ãƒ»ãƒ»ãªã„ ã€€ discer n è¦‹åˆ†ã‘ã‚‹ ã€€ white-tippe d å…ˆç«¯ã®ç™½ã„ ã€€ billo w å¤§æ³¢ ã€€ blen d æ··ã˜ã£ãŸã‚‚ã®


In fisher`s barque I onward glide

O`er the broad expanse of ocean`s tide,

And towards th`horizon when I turn

My glance I scarcely can discern

Where the white-tipped billows end,

That with the cloud-horizon blend.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


O`er the wide sea plain,

As I row and look around,

It appears to me

That the white waves, far away,

Are the ever shining sky.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The tumbling waters of a brawling stream fiercely hurl against the rocks and they whirls onward in double torrent.  In further flow they will show a single stream.

The brawling stream against the rock

Its tumbling waters fiercely hurls,

Divided by the furious shock,

In double torrent on-wards whirls:

In further flow

In true a single stream` twill show.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though a swift stream be

By a rock met and restrained

In impetuous flow,

Yet, divided, it speeds on,

And at last unites again.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The screaming sea-birds fly to and fro, night after night and their ceaseless cry scarcely allow the guard of Suma`s gate to sleep for a moment.

 

 

ãã„ãã„ã¨é³´ãæµ·é³¥ãŒå¤œãªå¤œãªã€ã‚ã¡ã“ã¡ã«é£›ã‚“ã§ã€ãã®çµ¶ãˆé–“ãªã„æ³£ãå£°ãŒã€é ˆç£¨ã®é–¢æ‰€ã®ç•ªäººãŒä¸€ç¬çœ ã‚‹ã“ã¨ã‚‚ã»ã¨ã‚“ã©è¨±ã•ãªã„ã€‚

Tween Awaji and Suma fly

The screaming sea-birds to and fro

Night after night; their ceaseless cry

Doth scarce a moment`s sleep allow,

To whom his fate

Allots the ward of Suma`s gate.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Guard of Suma`s Gate,

From your sleep, how many nights

Have you waked at cries

Of the plaintive sanderlings,

Migrant from Awaji`s isle?

(Translated by MacCauley)

When autumn chilly blast blows, the moonbeams peep through from amid the dark clouds that are drifting.  I love to see the bright edged shadows over the meadow. 

 

 

ç§‹ã®å†·ãŸã„çªé¢¨ãŒå¹ãã¨ãã€æ¼‚ã†æš—ã„é›²ã®é–“ã‹ã‚‰æœˆã®å…‰ãŒã®ããã€‚è‰åŸã®ä¸Šã«ã€æ˜ã‚‹ãç¸å–ã‚‰ã‚ŒãŸå½±ãŒè¦‹ãŸã„ã‚‚ã®ã ã€‚

When bloweth autumn`s chilly blast,

Through rifts at times the moonbeams peep,

From`mid the dark clouds drifting past,

And earth in pallid radiance steep,

I love to see

The bright-edged shadows o`er the lea.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


See, how clear and bright

Is the moon-light finding ways

`Mong the riven clouds

That, with drifting autumn-wind,

Gracefully float o`er the sky!

(Translated by MacCauley)

I fear you will break the covenant that you have sworn with me and your love will pass away from me.  My tress is as tangled as the unkempt mass that flow over my pillow.

I fear me thou wilt break the pact

Thou mad`st with me -- thy love will pass

Away from me, whom thoughts distract,

As tangled as the unkempt mass

My ravel tresses show.

That o`er my waking pillow flow.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If it be for aye

That he wills our love should last?

Ah! I do not know!

And this morn my anxious thoughts,

Like my black hair, are confused.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I heard a lesser cuckoo cry and I searched through all the echoing sky but I saw neither the bird nor the cloud.  The morning moon alone met my eye.

I heard the Hototogisu cry

I searched throughout the echoing sky`

No Hototogisu could espy

The morning moon but met my eye.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


When I turned my look
Toward the place whence I had heard
Hototogisu ,--
Lo! the only object there
Was the moon of early dawn.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

What a wretched life I have!  I strive with my sad lot, which leaves me no peace or rest.  My anguish is shown by the tears that flow down over my cheek.

What wretchedness is mine, O Life!

With what deep mis`ry tho`t oppress!

With my sad lot I strive in strife

That leaveth me nor peace nor rest;

The tears that flow

Down o`er the cheek my anguish show.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though in deep distress

(Through the cruel blow), my life

Still is left to me:--

But my tears I can not keep;

They can not my grief endure.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Over all the world the evil hold its sway and I fled far away amid the hills, but there too sad cries of deer thrill.

O`er the world both evil aye hold sway

I deemed, and for I fled away

Admit the hills:

But there, the deer`s sad cry, too thrills.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Ah! within the world,

Way of flight I find nowhere.

I had thought to hide

In the mountains` farthest depths;

Yet e`en there the stag`s cry sounds.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

If I were to linger in life longer, what I think to be a grievous life would seem to be a slight burden that I can bear almost with delight.

Were I to linger more in life

What seemed of old a grievous strife.

Would seem to be a burden slight,

To be borne almost with delight.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


If I long should live,

Then, perchance, the present days

May be dear to me;--

Just as past time fraught with grief

Now comes fondly back in thought.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I lay sleepless through the long night with wretched, distracted thought, and I waited for the light of daybreak through the chink of screen that guards my chamber.

With wretched thoughts distracted I

On sleepless pallet restless lay

The livelong night: with wistful eye

I waited for the breaking day

Through chink of screen

That guards my chamber -- peeping seen.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Now, - as through the night

Longingly I pass the hours,

And the day`s dawn lags, -

E`en my bedroom`s crannied doors

Heartless are, indeed, to me.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

My lovesick soul sways the moonlight night with deep melancholy.  See how my face betrays my sorrow and how tears roll down my cheek.

With deeper melancholy sways

The moonlit night my love-sick soul;

See how many face my woe betrays,

How down my cheek the tears roll.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Is it then the moon

That has made me sad, as though

It had bade me grieve?

Lifting up my troubled face,--

Ah! the tears, the (mournful) tears!

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

In autumn evening, the passing shower sweeps onward.  While its scattered drops are yet on yew-leaves, the rising mist is seen on yonder hillside.

 

 

ç§‹ã®å¤•æš®ã‚Œã«ã€é€šã‚Šéãã‚‹ã«ã‚ã‹é›¨ ãŒ( ã‚ãŸã‚Š ã‚’) æƒã„ã¦ã„ãã€‚ãã®æ•£ã‚‰ã•ã‚ŒãŸé›¨ç²’ãŒã¾ã ã‚¤ãƒã‚¤ã®è‘‰ã«æ®‹ã£ã¦ã„ã‚‹é–“ã«ã€å‘ã“ã†ã®å±±è…¹ã«ç«‹ã¡ä¸Šã‚‹éœ§ãŒè¦‹ãˆã‚‹ã€‚

èªå¥ ã€€ showe r ã«ã‚ã‹é›¨ ã€€ swee p æƒãã€æŠ¼ã—æµã™ ã€€ onwar d å…ˆ ã¸( é€²è¡Œã®ç¶™ç¶šã‚’ç¤º ã™ )  scatte r æ•£ã‚‰ã›ã‚‹ ã€€ dro p ã—ãšã ã€€ ye w ã‚¤ãƒã‚¤ã®æœ¨ã€ãƒ¨ãƒ¼ãƒ­ãƒƒãƒ‘ã‚¤ãƒã‚¤ ã€€ mis t éœ§ ã€€ yonde r ã‚ãã“ã®ã€å‘ã“ã†ã®


The passing shower on ward sweeps, -

Not yet upon the yew-leaves dried

Its scattered drops, - and lo! There creeps

The rising mist up yon hill-side

Of autumn e`en,

At twilight`s chilly hour seen.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Lo, an autumn eve!

See the deep vale`s mists arise

Mong the fir-tree`s leaves

That still hold the dripping wet

Of the (chill day`s) sudden showers.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

For but one night`s sake,

Short as is a node of reed

Grown in Naniwa bay,

Must I, henceforth, long for him

With my whole heart, till life`s close?

The thread of my life must snap off sooner or later and I am almost afraid of longer life.  Longer life may weaken my patience in sorrow, loneliness and misery.

Of my life or soon or late the thread,

The withering-thread perforce must snap:

I almost would` twere now, I dread

Of longer life the sure hap --

The secrete of our love display,

For e`er our happiness low laid.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Life! Thou string of gems!

If thou art to end, break now.

For, if yet I live,

All I do to hide (my love)

May at last grow weak (and fail).

(Translated by MacCauley)

I wish I would show you often-drenched sleeves of the island fishermen and their tints never fade by salt water.  But alas!  All colours passed away from me.

I would that I might show to thee

The inlaid-fisher`s oft-drenched sleeve,

I would that thine own eye might see

How salt waves their tints ne`er thieve;

From mine, alas!

Aye tear-bedewed, the colours pass.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Let me show him these!

E`en the fisherwomen`s sleeves

On Ojima`s shores,

Though wet through and wet again,

Do not change their dyer`s hues.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I hear grasshopper`s chirp all through night and hoarfrost covers the ground, and I strive in vain to get asleep in a lonely couch.

 

 

å¤œã®é–“ä¸­ã€ã‚­ãƒªã‚®ãƒªã‚¹ã®é³´ãã®ãŒèã“ãˆã¦ã€éœœãŒåœ°é¢ã‚’è¦†ã£ã¦ã„ã‚‹ã€‚ç§ã¯ä¸€äººã®å¯åºŠã§çœ ã‚ã†ã¨ã—ã¦ã„ã‚‹ãŒã€å¯ä»˜ã‘ãªã„ã§ã„ã‚‹ã€‚

Now grasshopper`s chirp in the livelong night

I hear now hoar-frost doth the ground

O` er carpet, and in sadden plight,

My day-worn raiment yer unbound,

I strive in vain.

On lonely couch repose to gain.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


On a chilling mat,

Drawing close my folded quilt,

I must sleep alone,

While all through the frosty night

Sounds a cricket`s (forlorn chirp).

(Translated by MacCauley)

Unseen as the rock never bared at the lowest ebb tide, my grief is unknown to anybody.  Who knows my tear-drenched sleeve will never dry?

My sleeve is as the rock unseen

Ne`er bared at lowest ebb of tide,

And none do guess my grief, I ween,

Now how my tear-drenched sleeve`s ne`er dried.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Like a rock at sea,

E`en at ebb-tide hid from view,

Is my tear-drenched sleeve:--

Never for a moment dry,

And unknown in human ken.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Throughout the endless life I wish to dwell in peace.  I watch a fishing boat that hauls abundant fish with nets.  How beautiful it is to me, and how pleasant such a life would be!

O that throughout an endless life,

I might in peace dwell far from strife!

For ever watching the fishing yawl,

And view the nets abundant haul:

How fair to me,

How pleasant such a lot would be!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Would that this, our world,

Might be ever as it is!

What a lovely scene!

See that fisherwoman`s boat,

Rope-drawn, rowed along the beach.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Now autumn wind sweeps down the steep of Yoshino and I hear the sound of beating of cloth, brought forth to me by the night wind, from my lovely village home.

Now autumn gusts sweep

Down Mi-yoshino `s steep,

And far into the night so drear

The sound of beating of the cloth,

Borne to me on the night-wind forth,

From my lonely village home, I hear.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


From Mount Yoshino

Blows a chill, autumnal wind,

In the deepening night.

Cold the ancient hamlet is;--

Sounds of beating cloth I hear.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

I am an ignorant man, unfit to lead all the multitude of men to salvation, but I would gladly wear again priest`s black robe and I am a humble dweller on this sacred mountain of Hiei.

An ignorant man am I, unfit

O`er all the multitude of men

In dignity supreme to sit:

The simple priests`black robe again

I would, a humble dweller on

Wagatatsusoma, gladly don.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Though I am not fit,

I have dared to shield the folk

Of this woeful world

With my black-dyed (sacred) sleeve:--

I, who live on Mount Hiei.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

The court is strewn with cherry flower as thick as if the drifted snow lay thereon, and I, too, shall lie low beneath the blow of man`s inevitable enemy.

The court with Sakura`s flower is strewn

As thick as though the drifted snow

Did thereon lay: and I too saw

As withered low shall lie `neath blow

Of man`s inevitable foe.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Not the snow of flowers,

That the hurrying wild-wind drags

Round the garden court,

Is it that here, withering, falls:--

That in truth is I, myself.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

On dusky hour of night, I waited for my longed-for mistress on the meeting place of Matsuho shore.  Why you are so late!  My ardent passion rages higher than the fire that heats the saltpans.

èªå¥ ã€€ dusk y è–„æš—ã„ã€é™°é¬±ãª ã€€ long fo r æ‹ç„¦ãŒã‚Œã‚‹ ã€€ mistres s æ„› äºº( å¥³ æ€§)ã€€ shor e å²¸è¾º ã€€ arden t ç†±çƒˆãª ã€€ passio n æƒ…ç†± ã€€ rag e æ¿€æ€’ã™ã‚‹ã€è’ã‚Œç‹‚ã† ã€€ saltpa n å¡©ç”°

 


On Mats`ho`s shore, our meeting place,

At dusky hour of night, I wait

My longed-for mistress to embrace;

Ah, why linger`st thou so late!

My ardent passion, than the fire

The heats the salt-pans, rages higher.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Like the salt sea-weed,

Burning in the evening calm,

On Matsuo`s shore,

All my being is aglow,

Waiting one who does not come.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

In dim twilight, the wind blows softly over the stream through the oak forest of the shrine.  The body purification in the water shows that the summer has not yet quite gone.

O`er Nara`s streamlet softly blow

The winds in the new dim twilight,

The Misogi, thereby set, show

That summer hath not yet gone quite.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


Lo! at Nara`s brook

Evening comes, and rustling winds

Stir the oak-trees` leave;--

Not a sign of summer left

But the sacred bathing there.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

Some men love me, and some hate me.  Whenever I think upon this miserable world, my fate seems to me more pitiful.

Some men love, some men me hate

Inspire: when e`er I think upon

This miserable world, my fate

More pitiable doth them to me.

 

(Translated by Dickins)


For some men I grieve;--

Some men are hateful to me;--

And this wretched world

To me, weighted down with care,

Is a place of misery.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)

A rent and tattered roof is seen in the Imperial Palace, where weeping ferns grow.  What a pity it is to see our prosperity has passed away!

Oh th`hundred-chambered palace lo

A rent and tattered roof is seen,

Where rank Shinobu weeds do grow: -

How long, how hard our pain hath been!

 

(Translated by Dickins)


O Imperial House!

When I think of former days,

How I long for thee!

More than e`en the clinging vines

Hanging `neath thine ancient eaves.

 

(Translated by MacCauley)
